Whitney anukramaс╣З─л

[K─Бn╠Дk─Бyana.тАФс╣гaс╕Нviс╣Е┼Ыakam. mantrokt─Бrbudidevatyam. ─Бnuс╣гс╣нubham: 1. 7-p. vir─Бс╣н ┼Ыakvar─л 3-av.; 3. paroс╣гс╣Зiс╕е; 4, 3-av. uс╣гс╣Зigbс╣Ыhat─лgarbh─Б par─Бtriс╣гс╣нup 6-p. atijagat─л; 9, 11, 14, 23, 26. pathy─Бpan╠Дkti; 15, 22, 24, 25. 3-av. 7-p. ┼Ыakvar─л; 16. 3-av. 5-p. vir─Бс╕Н upariс╣гс╣н─Бjjyotis triс╣гс╣нubh; 17. 3-p. g─Бyatr─л.]

Whitney

Comment

This and the following hymn are wanting in P─Бipp., although bits of vss. 15-17 of this one are to be found in P─Бipp. xvii. The opening words of the two are quoted together in K─Бu┼Ы. 16. 21, in connection with rites for insuring success in war. тМКThe use of the two hymns forms a sequel to the rites described in the introduction to viii. 8, which see; and cf. under viii. 8. 24.тМЛ

Translations

Translated: Ludwig, p. 530; Henry, 126, 164; Griffith, ii. 84; Bloomfield, 123, 631.

Griffith

An incantation for the destruction of a hostile army

Whitney
Translation
  1. What arms (b─Бh├║) [there are], what arrows, and the powers
    (v─лry├а) of bows, swords (as├н), axes (para┼Ы├║), weapon, and what
    thought-and-design in the heartтАФall that, O Arbudi, do thou make our
    enemies to see; and do thou show forth specters (ud─Бr├б).
Notes

The comm. refers to AB. vi. 1, where Arbuda is named and called a
serpent-sage, and declares Arbudi and Nyarbudi to be his two sons.
Ud─Бr─Бn he explains as udgat─Бn antarikс╣гacar─Бn rakс╣гaс╕еpi┼Ы─Бc─Бd─лn
mantras─Бmarthyodbh─Бvit─Бn, or also as s┼лryara┼Ыmiprabhav─Б ulk─Бdaya
─Бntarikс╣гy─Б utp─Бt─Бс╕е, specters or portents. тМКP─Бda d, below, vs. 13
b.тМЛ

Griffith

All arms and every arrow, all the power and might that bows possess, The warlike weapon, axes, swords, the plan and purpose in the heart, All this, O Arbudi, make thou visible to our enemies, and let them look on mist and fog.

Whitney
Translation
  1. The God that is Arbudi by name, and the lord (─л╠Б┼Ы─Бna) Nyarbudi, by
    whom the atmosphere is involved (─Б-vс╣Ы), and this great earthтАФby those
    (two) who are allied with Indra, I go after what is conquered with an
    army.
Notes

Probably ‘I follow up with my army what is already conquered by them.’
The two last p─Бdas are by the comm. reckoned as the first line of the
next verse.

Griffith

The God whose name is Arbudi, and Nyarbudi the Mighty One, The two by whom the air and this great earth are compassed and possessed, With these two friends of Indra I go forth to conquer with the host.

Translation
  1. Stand thou up, O god-folk, Arbudi, with the army; breaking (bha├▒j)

Notes

The comm. explains bhog├йbhis as ─Бtm─лy─Бiс╕е sarpa┼Ыar─лr─Бiс╕е.

Griffith

Rise with our army stand thou up. O Godlike Being, Arbudi. Breaking the hosts of enemies, surround them with thy winding coils.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Presenting to view, O Nyarbudi, the seven kinds of specters, with
    them all do thou stand up, when the butter is offered, with the army.
Notes

The pada-text reads in a j─Бt─Б╠Бn: niре░arbude; but the reading is
plainly false, and should be either j─Бt─Б╠Б: niре░arbude, or j─Бt─Б╠Бni:
arbude; either of these, considering that to the scribes nya and
nnya are entirely equivalent and exchangeable (see my Skt. Gr. ┬з┬з
229, 232), would correctly represent the saс╣Бhit─Б-reading. тМКCf. the
reading of the comm. at 10. 21.тМЛ

Griffith

Exhibiting, O Arbudi, seven children of the mist and fog, When butter hath been offered, rise with all of these and with the host.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Smiting herself, tear-faced, and crop-eared (?), let her yell, with
    disheveled hair, when the man is slain, bitten (? rad), O Arbudi, of
    thee.
Notes

‘Her’тАФnamely, the wife or sister or the like; more distinctly pointed to
in the next verse. Radita ought to mean rather ‘scraped’ or
‘scratched’; there seems to be no other example of it in the sense
‘bitten’: perhaps as a mere scratch from the fang of a serpent is enough
to kill. The comm. takes radita as a noun (like ruta, stnita, citta,
etc.), = dant─Бir vilekhane kh─Бdane sati. Of kс╣Ыdhukarс╣З#i the comm.
says: kс╣Ыdhv iti hrasvan─Бma: karс╣З─Бbharaс╣Зaparity─Бgena hrasvakarс╣З─л. The
verse is translated (also vs. 14, and 10. 7) by Bloomfield, in AJP. xi.
340.

Griffith

Beating her breast, with tearful face, let the short-earned, the wild-haired hag. Shriek loudly when a man is slain, pierced through by thee, O Arbudi;

Whitney
Translation
  1. Drawing in her kar┼л╠Бkara, seeking with her mind her son, husband,
    brother, also her people (sv├б)тАФin case of thy bite, O Arbudi.
Notes

The ending is the same with that of vs. 7, understood as the comm. takes
it; we might also supply ‘[he being] bitten’ etc. The Pet. Lex.
renders kar┼лkara ‘vertebra of the neck and spine’: rather (in ┼ЫB. xii.
2. 4^(10, 14)), perhaps, ‘a point or spinous process of a vertebra.’ The
comm. explains karu as an imitative word, and kar┼лkara as meaning
anything that makes the sound karu, and so designating
hastap─Бd─Бdyavayavagataс╣Б saс╣Бdhimad asthij─Бtam; and he goes on loke hi
bhayava┼Ы─Бd ubhayor hastayoс╕е paraspar─Бn╠Дgulinip─лс╕Нanena t─Бdс╣Ы┼Ыaс╣Б ┼Ыabdam
utp─Бdayanti. This is far from relieving satisfactorily the obscurity.
Most of our mss. accent sv─Б╠Аn in c.

Griffith

Snatching away the vertebra, while with her thought she seeks her son, Her husband, brother, kin, when one, Arbudi! hath been pierc- ed by thee.

режреп рдЕрд▓рд┐рдХреНрд▓рд╡рд╛ рдЬрд╛рд╖реНрдХрдорджрд╛ ...{Loading}...
Whitney
Translation
  1. Let the buzzards, j─Бс╣гkamad├бs, vultures, falcons, winged ones, let
    the crows, the birds (┼Ыak├║ni), satisfy themselvesтАФexhibiting among the
    enemiesтАФin case of thy bite, O Arbudi.
Notes

We have here two refrain-phrases, neither of which stands in any
grammatical connection with its surroundings (the pple. ’exhibiting’
being nom. sing. masc). The comm. reads in a aliklab─Бс╕е and y─Бс╕е
klamad─Бс╕е; and some of the mss. have j─Бс╕еkam- (so our B.O.s.m.).

Griffith

Let vultures, ravens, kites, and crows, and every carrion-eating bird. Feast on our foes, and show where one, Arbudi! hath been pierced by thee.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Then let all wild beasts, let the fly, let the worm satisfy itself
    upon the carrion of men, bitten, O Arbudi, of thee.
Notes

Here the refrain stands again in grammatical connection.

Griffith

Then let each greedy beast of prey, and fly and worm regale itself Upon the human corpse where one, Arbudi, hath been pierced by thee.

Translation
  1. Let their arms be confounded, and what thought-and-design is in
    their heart; let not anything of them be leftтАФin case of thy bite, O

Griffith

Let those mens’ arms grow faint and weak, dull be the purpose of their heart; And let not aught of them be left when thou, O Arbudi, hast pierced.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Smiting themselves let them (f.) run together, smiting on the
    breast, the thighs (? paс╣н─Бur├б), not anointing, with disheveled hair,
    wailing when the man is slain, bitten, O Arbudi, of thee.
Notes

Translated by Bloomfield, ib. (see vs. 7). I follow both translators in
rendering paс╣н─Бur├б by ’thigh,’ although it is not too acceptable,
considering the familiarity of ┼лru as name for ’thigh.’ SPP. reads
instead paс╣н┼лr─Б├║, with a very small minority of his mss. (of ours, only
B.s.m. has it), and with the comm. The latter defines it simply as
tat- (i.e. uraс╕е) prade┼Ы─Бu. He makes agh─Бrin from agha and root
с╣Ы: aghena bhartс╣Ыviyogajanitena duс╕еkhen─Б тАЭrt─Бс╕е!

Griffith

Self-smiting, beating breast and thigh, careless of unguent, with their hair dishevelled, weeping, hags shall run together, when a man is slain, when thou, O Arbudi, hast pierced.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Dog-accompanied Apsarases, she-jackals (? r┼л╠Бpak─Б) also, O Arbudi,
    the ri┼Ы─Б╠Б, licking much in the inner vessel, seeking what is
    ill-depositedтАФall these (f.), O Arbudi, do thou make our enemies to see,
    and do thou show forth specters;тАФ
Notes

The conclusion is nearly the same with vs. 1 e, f, g, above, and is
also repeated below тМКvss. 22, 24тМЛ. The accent of ┼Ыv├аnvat─лs seems
certainly wrong, but it is read by all the mss., and avouched by the
commentary to Pr─Бt. iii. 73. The translation of r┼лpak─Б is that of the
minor Pet. Lex., apparently founded solely on an Avestan analogue; the
comm. defines it as m─Бy─Бva┼Ы─Бt kevalaс╣Б r┼лpam─Бtreс╣Зo тАЩpalabhyam─Бn─Бс╕е
sen─Бr┼лpak─Бс╕е. He reads antaс╕е and p─Бtre as two independent words,
according to his custom of caring nothing for accent. For ri┼Ы─Бm
(“tearing one, as designating some small animal,” minor Pet. Lex.) he
reads va┼Ы─Бm ‘cow,’ so that we lose any light he might have cast on the
obscure description. Bp. reads riс╣г─Б╠Бm. Pr─Бt. iii. 75 and iv. 77
prescribe the pada-reading durnihita-.

Griffith

Apsarases with dog-like mates, and Rupakas, O Arbudi, And her who licks the cup inside, and seeks to wound in ill- kept place, All these, O Arbudi, do thou make visible to our enemies and let them look on mists and fog.

рдкрдгреНрдбрд┐рдд рдХреНрд╖реЗрдордХрд░рдгрджрд╛рд╕ рддреНрд░рд┐рд╡реЗрджреА - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

Whitney
Translation
  1. Let our enemy lie squelched, crushed, slain, O Nyarbudi; let tongues
    of fire, tufts of smoke, go conquering with the army.
Notes

The comm. reads in a pravl─лnas, in accordance with the more usual
form.

Griffith

Stifled and crushed, O Nyarbudi, low let the smitten foeman lie. With tongue of fire and crest of smoke go conquering maidens with our host!

Whitney
Translation
  1. Of our enemies, pushed forth by it, O Arbudi, let Indra, lord of
    might (┼Ы├бc─лp├бti), slay each best man (v├бra); let no one soever of
    them be freed.
Notes

‘By it’тАФi.e. by the army; the comm. reads instead tvay─Б ‘by thee.’
With a, b compare vi. 67. 2 c, d. тМКOur d occurs several
times: see note to iii. 19. 8.тМЛ

Griffith

May Indra, Lord of Might, strike down each bravest warrior of the foes, Whom this our band hath put to flight: let not one man of those escape.

Translation
  1. Let their hearts burst open (ut-kas), their breath pass up aloft;
    let dryness of mouth follow after our enemies, тМКandтМЛ not those who are

equivalently.

Griffith

Let their hearts burst asunder, let their breath fly up and pass away. Let dryness of the mouth o’ertake our foemen, not the friendly ones.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Both they who are wise (dh─л╠Бra) and they who are unwise, those
    going away and they who are deaf, they of darkness arid they who are
    hornless (t┼лpar├б), likewise those that smell of (?) the goatтАФall
    those (m.), O Arbudi, do thou make our enemies to see, and do thou show
    forth specters.
Notes

The meaning of -abhiv─Бsin is wholly uncertain тМКcf. P─Бli v─БsitaтМЛ; the
Pet. Lex. conjectures instead -abhiv─Б┼Ыin, and the comm. reads
bast─Бviv─Б┼Ыin, as from basta + avi + v─Б┼Ыin. He also, in defiance of
pada-text and accent, renders tamas─Б╠Бs as t├бmas─Б. тМКCf. nabhas├б-s
(not n├бbhas-as), ix. 4. 22.тМЛ

Griffith

The clever and the foolish ones, those who are twisted round, the deaf, The dusky-hued, the hornless goats and those whose voice is like the buck’s, All these, O Arbudi, do thou make visible to our enemies: cause them to look on mists and fog.

Translation
  1. Let both Arbudi and Trishandhi pierce through our enemies, in order
    that, O Indra, Vс╣Ыitra-slayer, lord of might, we may slay of them, of our
    enemies, by thousands.
Notes

Triс╣гandhi, lit. ‘of three joints,’ is conspicuous especially in the
next hymn. The comm. explains it here as ka┼Ыcit sen─Бmohako devaс╕е
saс╣Бdhitrayopetavajr─Бyudh─Бbhim─Бn─л v─Б.

Griffith

Arbudi and Trishandhi fall upon our foes and scatter them, So that, O Indra, Lord of Might, Slayer of Vritra, we may kill thousands of these our enemies!

Translation
  1. The forest-trees, them of the forest-trees, herbs and plants,
    Gandharvas-and-Apsarases, serpents, gods, pure-folk (puс╣Зyajan├б).
    FathersтАФall those, O Arbudi, do thou make our enemies to see, and do
    thou show forth specters.

The comm. identifies the ‘pure-folk’ with the yakс╣гas. тМКWith c, d,
cf. viii. 8. 15, above.тМЛ тМКCf. K─Бu┼Ы. 73. 5.тМЛ

Griffith

Tall trees, and those who live in woods, the herbs and creeping plants of Earth, Gandharvas, and Apsarases, Snakes, Beings, Fathers, Gods, All these do thou, O Arbudi, make visible to our enemies: cause them to look on mists and fog.

Griffith

High sway have Maruts, and the God Aditya, Brahmanaspati, High sway have Indra, Agni, and Dilator, Mitra, Prajapati, High sway have Rishis given to you, showing upon our enemies where thou, O Arbudi, hast pierced.
